<p>The <b>polar bear</b> (<i><b>Ursus maritimus</b></i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Hypercarnivore" title="Hypercarnivore">hypercarnivorous</a> <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bear</a> whose native range lies largely within the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, encompassing the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, its surrounding seas and surrounding land masses. It is the largest <a href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> bear species, as well as the largest extant land carnivore.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> A boar (adult male) weighs around 350–700&nbsp;kg (770–1,540&nbsp;lb),<sup id="cite_ref-Animal_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Animal-8">[8]</a></sup> while a sow (adult female) is about half that size. Although it is the <a href="/wiki/Sister_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Sister species">sister species</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bear</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MSW3_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-9">[9]</a></sup> it has evolved to occupy a narrower <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a>, with many body characteristics adapted for cold temperatures, for moving across snow, ice and open water, and for hunting <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, which make up most of its diet.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Although most polar bears are born on land, they spend most of their time on the <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a>. Their scientific name means "<a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">maritime</a> bear" and derives from this fact. Polar bears hunt their preferred food of seals from the edge of sea ice, often living off fat reserves when no sea ice is present. Because of their dependence on the sea ice, polar bears are classified as <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StirlingGuravich1998_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StirlingGuravich1998-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of expected <a href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitat loss">habitat loss</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, the polar bear is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable species</a>. For decades, large-scale hunting raised international concern for the future of the species, but populations rebounded after controls and quotas began to take effect.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> For thousands of years, the polar bear has been a key figure in the material, spiritual, and cultural life of <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">circumpolar peoples</a>, and polar bears remain important in their cultures. Historically, the polar bear has also been known as the "white bear".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> It is sometimes referred to as the "nanook", based on the <a href="/wiki/Inuit_languages" title="Inuit languages">Inuit</a> term <i>nanuq</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_and_etymology">Naming and etymology</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Constantine_Phipps,_2nd_Baron_Mulgrave" title="Constantine Phipps, 2nd Baron Mulgrave">Constantine John Phipps</a> was the first to describe the polar bear as a distinct species in 1774 in his report about his <a href="/wiki/1773_Phipps_expedition_towards_the_North_Pole" title="1773 Phipps expedition towards the North Pole">1773 expedition towards the North Pole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Phipps1774_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phipps1774-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup> He chose the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific name">scientific name</a> <i>Ursus maritimus</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "maritime bear",<sup id="cite_ref-LatDic_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LatDic-15">[15]</a></sup> due to the animal's native habitat. The <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> refer to the animal as <i><a href="/wiki/Nanook" title="Nanook">nanook</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliterated</a> as <i>nanuq</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Inupiat_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Inupiat language">Inupiat language</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yupik_peoples" title="Yupik peoples">Yupik</a> also refer to the bear as <i>nanuuk</i> in <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Yupik_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Siberian Yupik language">Siberian Yupik</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The bear is <i>umka</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_language" title="Chukchi language">Chukchi language</a>. In Russian, it is usually called бе́лый медве́дь (<i>bélyj medvédj</i>, the white bear), though an older word still in use is ошку́й (<i>Oshkúj</i>, which comes from the <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a> <i>oski</i>, "bear").<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In Quebec, the polar bear is referred to as <i>ours blanc</i> ("white bear") or <i>ours polaire</i> ("polar bear").<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In the Norwegian-administered <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a> archipelago, the polar bear is referred to as <i>Isbjørn</i> ("ice bear").
</p><p>The polar bear was previously considered to be in its own <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a>, <i>Thalarctos</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Liddell_1980_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liddell_1980-20">[20]</a></sup> However, evidence of hybrids between <a href="/wiki/Ursid_hybrid" title="Ursid hybrid">polar bears and brown bears</a>, and of the recent evolutionary divergence of the two species, does not support the establishment of this separate genus, and the accepted scientific name is now therefore <i>Ursus maritimus</i>, as Phipps originally proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polarbearonice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Polarbearonice.jpg/220px-Polarbearonice.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Polarbearonice.jpg/330px-Polarbearonice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Polarbearonice.jpg/440px-Polarbearonice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="338"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polarbearonice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Polar bears have evolved adaptations for Arctic life. For example, large furry feet and short, sharp, stocky claws give them good traction on ice.</div></div></div>
<p>The bear <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ursidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ursidae">Ursidae</a>, is thought to have split from other carnivorans about 38&nbsp;million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The subfamily <a href="/wiki/Ursinae" title="Ursinae">Ursinae</a> originated approximately 4.2&nbsp;million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The oldest known polar bear <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> is a 130,000 to 110,000-year-old jaw bone, found on <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles_Foreland" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles Foreland">Prince Charles Foreland</a> in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindqvist_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindqvist-23">[23]</a></sup> Fossils show that between 10,000 and 20,000 years ago, the polar bear's <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molar teeth</a> changed significantly from those of the brown bear.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-24">[24]</a></sup> Polar bears are thought to have diverged from a population of brown bears that became isolated during a period of glaciation in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a><sup id="cite_ref-DeMaster1981_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeMaster1981-5">[5]</a></sup> from the eastern part of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a> and the Kolym Peninsula).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The evidence from DNA analysis is more complex. The <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) of the polar bear diverged from the brown bear, <i>Ursus arctos</i>, roughly 150,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindqvist_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindqvist-23">[23]</a></sup> Further, some <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> of brown bear, as assessed by their mtDNA, were thought to be more closely related to polar bears than to other brown bears,<sup id="cite_ref-Waits_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waits-25">[25]</a></sup> meaning that the brown bear might not be considered a species under some <a href="/wiki/Species_concepts" class="mw-redirect" title="Species concepts">species concepts</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The mtDNA of extinct Irish brown bears is particularly close to polar bears.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> A comparison of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_genome" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear genome">nuclear genome</a> of polar bears with that of brown bears revealed a different pattern, the two forming genetically distinct clades that diverged approximately 603,000 years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-Hailer_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hailer-28">[28]</a></sup> although the latest research is based on analysis of the complete genomes (rather than just the mitochondria or partial nuclear genomes) of polar and brown bears, and establishes the divergence of polar and brown bears at 400,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Liu_et_al_2014_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liu_et_al_2014-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the two species have mated intermittently for all that time, most likely coming into contact with each other during warming periods, when polar bears were driven onto land and brown bears migrated northward. Most brown bears have about 2 percent genetic material from polar bears, but one population, the <a href="/wiki/ABC_Islands_bears" class="mw-redirect" title="ABC Islands bears">ABC Islands bears</a>, has between 5 percent and 10 percent polar bear genes, indicating more frequent and recent mating.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes-30">[30]</a></sup> Polar bears can breed with brown bears to produce fertile <a href="/wiki/Grizzly%E2%80%93polar_bear_hybrid" title="Grizzly–polar bear hybrid">grizzly–polar bear hybrids</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-DeMaster1981_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeMaster1981-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schliebe_2006_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schliebe_2006-31">[31]</a></sup> rather than indicating that they have only recently diverged, the new evidence suggests more frequent mating has continued over a longer period of time, and thus the two bears remain genetically similar.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes-30">[30]</a></sup> However, because neither species can survive long in the other's ecological niche, and because they have different <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, social and feeding behaviours, and other <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypic</a> characteristics, the two bears are generally classified as separate species.<sup id="cite_ref-first_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the polar bear was originally documented, two <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> were identified: the American polar bear (<i>Ursus maritimus maritimus</i>) by Constantine J. Phipps in 1774, and the Siberian polar bear (<i>Ursus maritimus marinus</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Simon_Pallas" title="Peter Simon Pallas">Peter Simon Pallas</a> in 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This distinction has since been invalidated.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wilson1976_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson1976-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-paetkau1999_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paetkau1999-36">[36]</a></sup> One alleged fossil subspecies has been identified: <i><a href="/wiki/Ursus_maritimus_tyrannus" title="Ursus maritimus tyrannus">Ursus maritimus tyrannus</a></i>, which became extinct during the Pleistocene. <i>U.m. tyrannus</i> was significantly larger than the living subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-DeMaster1981_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeMaster1981-5">[5]</a></sup> However, recent reanalysis of the fossil suggests that it was actually a brown bear.<sup id="cite_ref-polarresearch_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-polarresearch-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_and_distribution">Population and distribution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/220px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/330px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/440px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1575"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bears investigate the submarine <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Honolulu_(SSN-718)" title="USS Honolulu (SSN-718)">USS Honolulu</a></i> 450 kilometres (280&nbsp;mi) from the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a></div></div></div>
<p>The polar bear is found in the Arctic Circle and adjacent land masses as far south as <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(Island)" class="mw-redirect" title="Newfoundland (Island)">Newfoundland</a>. Due to the absence of human development in its remote habitat, it retains more of its original range than any other <a href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-derocher2004_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derocher2004-37">[37]</a></sup> While they are rare north of 88°, there is evidence that they range all the way across the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, and as far south as <a href="/wiki/James_Bay" title="James Bay">James Bay</a> in Canada. Their southernmost range is near the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">subarctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> zones. They can occasionally <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift</a> widely with the sea ice, and there have been anecdotal sightings as far south as <a href="/wiki/Berlev%C3%A5g" title="Berlevåg">Berlevåg</a> on the Norwegian mainland and the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">Sea of Okhotsk</a>. It is difficult to estimate a global population of polar bears as much of the range has been poorly studied; however, biologists use a working estimate of about 20–25,000 or 22–31,000 polar bears worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PBI_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBI-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the discovery of a southeast Greenland population in 2022,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> there are 20 generally recognized, discrete subpopulations of polar bears.<sup id="cite_ref-PBI_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBI-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PBSG14_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSG14-42">[42]</a></sup> The subpopulations display seasonal fidelity to particular areas, but <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> studies show that they are not reproductively isolated.<sup id="cite_ref-paetkau1999_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paetkau1999-36">[36]</a></sup> The 14 North American subpopulations range from the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Sea" title="Beaufort Sea">Beaufort Sea</a> south to <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> and east to eastern <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and account for about 54% of the global population.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><span class="mw-tmh-player video"><video id="mwe_player_0_placeholder" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Play_fight_of_polar_bears_edit_1.ogv/220px--Play_fight_of_polar_bears_edit_1.ogv.jpg" preload="none" class="thumbimage" width="220" height="165" data-durationhint="39" data-mwtitle="Play_fight_of_polar_bears_edit_1.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" disabled="disabled" tabindex="-1" style="aspect-ratio: 220 / 165;"></video><a class="mw-tmh-play" href="/wiki/File:Play_fight_of_polar_bears_edit_1.ogv" title="Play media" role="button"><span class="mw-tmh-play-icon"></span></a><span class="mw-tmh-duration mw-tmh-label" aria-label="39 seconds">0:39</span></span>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Play_fight_of_polar_bears_edit_1.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bears play-fighting</div></div></div>
<p>The usual range includes the territory of five nations: <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>), <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (<a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>), <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (<a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. These five nations are the signatories of the <a href="/wiki/International_Agreement_on_the_Conservation_of_Polar_Bears" class="mw-redirect" title="International Agreement on the Conservation of Polar Bears">International Agreement on the Conservation of Polar Bears</a>, which mandates cooperation on research and conservation efforts throughout the polar bear's range.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Bears sometimes swim to <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> from Greenland—about 600 sightings since the country's settlement in the 9th century, and five in the 21st century as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Polar_bear&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>—and are always killed because of their danger, as well as the cost and difficulty of repatriation.<sup id="cite_ref-im20160718_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-im20160718-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern methods of tracking polar bear populations have been implemented only since the mid-1980s, and are expensive to perform consistently over a large area. The most accurate counts require flying a helicopter in the <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_Arctic" title="Climate of the Arctic">Arctic climate</a> to find polar bears, shooting a tranquilizer dart at the bear to sedate it, and then tagging the bear. In <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, some Inuit have reported increases in bear sightings around human settlements in recent years, leading to a belief that populations are increasing. Scientists have responded by noting that hungry bears may be congregating around human settlements, leading to the illusion that populations are higher than they actually are.<sup id="cite_ref-campbell_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-46">[46]</a></sup> The Polar Bear Specialist Group of the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Species_Survival_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN Species Survival Commission">IUCN Species Survival Commission</a> takes the position that "estimates of subpopulation size or <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_yield" title="Sustainable yield">sustainable harvest</a> levels should not be made solely on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_ecological_knowledge" title="Traditional ecological knowledge">traditional ecological knowledge</a> without supporting scientific studies."<sup id="cite_ref-pbsgpr_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbsgpr-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of the 19 polar bear subpopulations recognized in 2017, one was in decline, two were increasing, seven were stable, and nine had insufficient data.<sup id="cite_ref-pbsg2017_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbsg2017-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg/220px-Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg/330px-Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg/440px-Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1280"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear_AdF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Polar bear jumping on <a href="/wiki/Fast_ice" title="Fast ice">fast ice</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spitsbergen" title="Spitsbergen">Spitsbergen</a> Island, <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a> Archipelago, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a></div></div></div>
<p>The polar bear is a <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammal</a> because it spends many months of the year at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-stirling1988intro_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1988intro-49">[49]</a></sup> However, it is the only living marine mammal with powerful, large limbs and feet that allow them to cover kilometres on foot and run on land.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Its preferred habitat is the annual <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> covering the waters over the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> and the Arctic inter-island <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a>. These areas, known as the "Arctic ring of life", have high biological productivity in comparison to the deep waters of the high Arctic.<sup id="cite_ref-derocher2004_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derocher2004-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-distribution_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distribution-51">[51]</a></sup> The polar bear tends to frequent areas where sea ice meets water, such as <a href="/wiki/Polynya" title="Polynya">polynyas</a> and leads (temporary stretches of open water in Arctic ice), to hunt the seals that make up most of its diet.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Freshwater is limited in these environments because it is either locked up in snow or saline. Polar bears are able to produce water through the metabolism of fats found in seal blubber,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and are therefore found primarily along the perimeter of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">polar ice pack</a>, rather than in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Basin" title="Arctic Basin">Polar Basin</a> close to the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> where the density of seals is low.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews15_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews15-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Annual ice contains areas of water that appear and disappear throughout the year as the weather changes. Seals migrate in response to these changes, and polar bears must follow their prey. In <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Bay" title="James Bay">James Bay</a>, and some other areas, the ice melts completely each summer (an event often referred to as "ice-floe breakup"), forcing polar bears to go onto land and wait through the months until the next freeze-up.<sup id="cite_ref-distribution_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distribution-51">[51]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi</a> and Beaufort seas, polar bears retreat each summer to the ice further north that remains frozen year-round.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output swimming</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg/200px-Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg/300px-Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg/400px-Ursus_maritimus_ranua_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A female bear at the <a href="/wiki/Ranua_Zoo" class="mw-redirect" title="Ranua Zoo">Ranua Zoo</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The only other bear of comparable size to the polar bear is the <a href="/wiki/Kodiak_bear" title="Kodiak bear">Kodiak bear</a>, which is a subspecies of <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bear</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kodiak_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kodiak-55">[55]</a></sup> Adult male polar bears weigh 350–700&nbsp;kg (770–1,500&nbsp;lb) and measure 2.4–3 metres (7&nbsp;ft 10&nbsp;in&nbsp;– 9&nbsp;ft 10&nbsp;in) in total length.<sup id="cite_ref-hemstock4_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock4-56">[56]</a></sup> Around the Beaufort Sea, however, mature males reportedly average 450&nbsp;kg (1,000&nbsp;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Adult females are roughly half the size of males and normally weigh 150–250&nbsp;kg (330–550&nbsp;lb), measuring 1.8–2.4 metres (5&nbsp;ft 11&nbsp;in&nbsp;– 7&nbsp;ft 10&nbsp;in) in length. Elsewhere, a slightly larger estimated average weight of 260&nbsp;kg (570&nbsp;lb) was claimed for adult females.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> When pregnant, however, females can weigh as much as 500&nbsp;kg (1,100&nbsp;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-hemstock4_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock4-56">[56]</a></sup> The polar bear is among the most <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a> of mammals, surpassed only by the <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">pinnipeds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">elephant seals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-perrin_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perrin-59">[59]</a></sup> The largest polar bear on record, reportedly weighing 1,002&nbsp;kg (2,209&nbsp;lb), was a male shot at <a href="/wiki/Kotzebue_Sound" title="Kotzebue Sound">Kotzebue Sound</a> in northwestern Alaska in 1960. This specimen, when mounted, stood 3.39&nbsp;m (11&nbsp;ft 1&nbsp;in) tall on its hindlegs. The shoulder height of an adult polar bear is 122 to 160&nbsp;cm (4&nbsp;ft 0&nbsp;in to 5&nbsp;ft 3&nbsp;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> While all bears are short-tailed, the polar bear's tail is relatively the shortest amongst living bears, ranging from 7 to 13&nbsp;cm (2.8 to 5.1&nbsp;in) in length.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Compared with its closest relative, the brown bear, the polar bear has a more elongated body build and a longer skull and nose.<sup id="cite_ref-first_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first-32">[32]</a></sup> As predicted by <a href="/wiki/Allen%27s_rule" title="Allen's rule">Allen's rule</a> for a northerly animal, the legs are stocky and the ears and tail are small.<sup id="cite_ref-first_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first-32">[32]</a></sup> However, the feet are very large to distribute load when walking on snow or thin ice and to provide propulsion when swimming; they may measure 30&nbsp;cm (12&nbsp;in) across in an adult.<sup id="cite_ref-lw2_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw2-63">[63]</a></sup> The pads of the paws are covered with small, soft papillae (dermal bumps), which provide traction on the ice. The polar bear's claws are short and stocky compared to those of the brown bear, perhaps to serve the former's need to grip heavy prey and ice.<sup id="cite_ref-first_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first-32">[32]</a></sup> The claws are deeply scooped on the underside which assists in digging in the ice of the natural habitat. Research of injury patterns in polar bear forelimbs found injuries to the right forelimb to be more frequent than those to the left, suggesting, perhaps, right-handedness.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Unlike the brown bear, polar bears in captivity are rarely overweight or particularly large, possibly as a reaction to the warm conditions of most zoos.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The 42 teeth of a polar bear reflect its highly carnivorous diet. The cheek teeth are smaller and more jagged than in the brown bear, and the <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canines</a> are larger and sharper. The <a href="/wiki/Dental_formula" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental formula">dental formula</a> is <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.4.2</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.4.3</span></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-first_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polar bears are superbly insulated by up to 10&nbsp;cm (4&nbsp;in) of <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-lw2_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw2-63">[63]</a></sup> their hide and their fur. Polar bear fur consists of a layer of dense underfur and an outer layer of <a href="/wiki/Guard_hair" title="Guard hair">guard hairs</a>, which appear white to tan but are actually transparent.<sup id="cite_ref-lw2_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw2-63">[63]</a></sup> Two genes that are known to influence melanin production, LYST and AIM1, are both mutated in polar bears, possibly leading to the absence on this pigment in their fur.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The guard hair is 5–15&nbsp;cm (2–6&nbsp;in) over most of the body.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Polar bears gradually <a href="/wiki/Moult" class="mw-redirect" title="Moult">moult</a> from May to August,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> but, unlike other Arctic mammals, they do not shed their coat for a darker shade to provide camouflage in summer conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Churchill_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill-68">[68]</a></sup> The hollow guard hairs of a polar bear coat were once thought to act as fiber-optic tubes to conduct light to its black skin, where it could be absorbed; however, this hypothesis was disproved by a study in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The white coat usually yellows with age. When kept in captivity in warm, humid conditions, the fur may turn a pale shade of green due to <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> growing inside the guard hairs.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Males have significantly longer hairs on their forelegs, which increase in length until the bear reaches 14 years of age. The male's ornamental foreleg hair is thought to attract females, serving a similar function to the <a href="/wiki/Lion#Mane" title="Lion">lion's mane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dimorph_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dimorph-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The polar bear has an extremely well developed sense of <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Olfaction">smell</a>, being able to detect seals nearly 1.6&nbsp;km (1&nbsp;mi) away and buried under 1&nbsp;m (3&nbsp;ft) of snow. Its hearing is about as acute as that of a human, and its vision is also good at long distances.<sup id="cite_ref-rosing20-23_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosing20-23-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The polar bear is an excellent <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_locomotion" title="Aquatic locomotion">swimmer</a> and often will swim for days.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> One bear swam continuously for 9 days in the frigid <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">Bering Sea</a> for 700&nbsp;km (400&nbsp;mi) to reach ice far from land. She then travelled another 1,800&nbsp;km (1,100&nbsp;mi). During the swim, the bear lost 22% of her body mass and her yearling cub died.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> With its body fat providing buoyancy, the bear swims in a <a href="/wiki/Dog_paddle" title="Dog paddle">dog paddle</a> fashion using its large forepaws for propulsion. Polar bears can swim at 10&nbsp;km/h (6&nbsp;mph). When walking, the polar bear tends to have a lumbering gait and maintains an average speed of around 5.6&nbsp;km/h (3.5&nbsp;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-behavior_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-behavior-75">[75]</a></sup> When sprinting, they can reach up to 40&nbsp;km/h (25&nbsp;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_history_and_behaviour">Life history and behaviour</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG/220px-Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG/330px-Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG/440px-Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1054"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bears_Play_fight.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Subadult males frequently play-fight. During the mating season, actual fighting is intense and often leaves scars or broken teeth.</div></div></div>
<p>Unlike brown bears, polar bears are not <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorial</a>. Although stereotyped as being voraciously aggressive, they are normally cautious in confrontations, and often choose to escape rather than fight.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews2729_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews2729-77">[77]</a></sup> Satiated polar bears rarely attack humans unless severely provoked.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, due to their lack of prior human interaction, hungry polar bears are extremely unpredictable, fearless towards people and are known to kill and sometimes eat humans.<sup id="cite_ref-bruemmer2533_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruemmer2533-80">[80]</a></sup> Many attacks by <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bears</a> are the result of surprising the animal, which is not the case with the polar bear. Polar bears are stealth hunters, and the victim is often unaware of the bear's presence until the attack is underway. Whereas brown bears often maul a person and then leave, polar bear attacks are more likely to be predatory and are almost always fatal.<sup id="cite_ref-conflicts_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conflicts-81">[81]</a></sup> However, due to the very small human population around the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, such attacks are rare. <a href="/wiki/Michio_Hoshino" title="Michio Hoshino">Michio Hoshino</a>, a Japanese wildlife photographer, was once pursued briefly by a hungry male polar bear in northern Alaska. According to Hoshino, the bear started running but Hoshino made it to his truck. The bear was able to reach the truck and tore one of the doors off the truck before Hoshino was able to drive off.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, adult polar bears live solitary lives. Yet, they have often been seen playing together for hours at a time and even sleeping in an embrace,<sup id="cite_ref-bruemmer2533_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruemmer2533-80">[80]</a></sup> and polar bear zoologist Nikita Ovsianikov has described adult males as having "well-developed friendships."<sup id="cite_ref-matthews2729_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews2729-77">[77]</a></sup> Cubs are especially playful as well. Among young males in particular, play-fighting may be a means of practicing for serious competition during mating seasons later in life.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Polar bears are usually quiet but do communicate with various sounds and vocalizations. Females communicate with their young with moans and chuffs, and the distress calls of both cubs and subadults consists of bleats.<sup id="cite_ref-Naughton_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naughton-84">[84]</a></sup> Cubs may hum while nursing.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> When nervous, bears produce huffs, chuffs and snorts while hisses, growls and roars are signs of aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-Naughton_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naughton-84">[84]</a></sup> Chemical communication can also be important: bears leave behind their scent in their tracks which allow individuals to keep track of one another in the vast Arctic wilderness.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, a photographer near Churchill took a now widely circulated set of photographs of a polar bear playing with a <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Eskimo_Dog" title="Canadian Eskimo Dog">Canadian Eskimo Dog</a> (<i>Canis familiaris</i>) a tenth of its size.<sup id="cite_ref-rosing128132_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosing128132-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The pair wrestled harmlessly together each afternoon for 10 days in a row for no apparent reason, although the bear may have been trying to demonstrate its friendliness in the hope of sharing the kennel's food. This kind of social interaction is uncommon; it is far more typical for polar bears to behave aggressively towards dogs.<sup id="cite_ref-rosing128132_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosing128132-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting_and_diet">Hunting and diet</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg/220px-Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg/330px-Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg/440px-Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="781" data-file-height="536"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polarbear_spitzbergen_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The polar bear's long muzzle and neck help it to search in deep holes for seals, while its powerful hindquarters enable it to drag massive prey<sup id="cite_ref-matthews73-88_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews73-88-89">[89]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The polar bear is the most carnivorous member of the bear family, and throughout most of its range, its diet primarily consists of <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed</a> (<i>Pusa hispida</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Bearded_seal" title="Bearded seal">bearded seals</a> (<i>Erignathus barbatus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The Arctic is home to millions of seals, which become prey when they surface in holes in the ice in order to breathe, or when they haul out on the ice to rest.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews73-88_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews73-88-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-91">[91]</a></sup> Polar bears hunt primarily at the interface between ice, water, and air; they only rarely catch seals on land or in open water.<sup id="cite_ref-amstrup2007_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amstrup2007-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The polar bear's most common hunting method is called <i>still-hunting</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-hemstock24-27_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock24-27-93">[93]</a></sup> the bear uses its excellent sense of smell to locate a seal breathing hole, and crouches nearby in silence for a seal to appear. The bear may lie in wait for several hours. When the seal exhales, the bear smells its breath, reaches into the hole with a forepaw, and drags it out onto the ice. The polar bear kills the seal by biting its head to crush its skull. The polar bear also hunts by stalking seals resting on the ice: upon spotting a seal, it walks to within 90&nbsp;m (100&nbsp;yd), and then crouches. If the seal does not notice, the bear creeps to within 9 to 12&nbsp;m (30 to 40&nbsp;ft) of the seal and then suddenly rushes forth to attack.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews73-88_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews73-88-89">[89]</a></sup> A third hunting method is to raid the birth lairs that female seals create in the snow.<sup id="cite_ref-hemstock24-27_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock24-27-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>A widespread legend tells that polar bears cover their black noses with their paws when hunting. This behaviour, if it happens, is rare – although the story exists in the <a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">oral history</a> of northern peoples and in accounts by early <a href="/wiki/Arctic_exploration" title="Arctic exploration">Arctic explorers</a>, there is no record of an eyewitness account of the behaviour in recent decades.<sup id="cite_ref-behavior_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-behavior-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_bear_(Ursus_maritimus)_with_its_prey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_its_prey.jpg/220px-Polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_its_prey.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_its_prey.jpg/330px-Polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_its_prey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_its_prey.jpg/440px-Polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_its_prey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2745" data-file-height="1544"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_bear_(Ursus_maritimus)_with_its_prey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bear feeding on a <a href="/wiki/Bearded_seal" title="Bearded seal">bearded seal</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mature bears tend to eat only the calorie-rich skin and blubber of the seal, which are highly digestible,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> whereas younger bears consume the protein-rich red meat.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews73-88_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews73-88-89">[89]</a></sup> Studies have also photographed polar bears scaling near-vertical cliffs, to eat birds' chicks and eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_(organism)" title="Juvenile (organism)">subadult</a> bears, which are independent of their mother but have not yet gained enough experience and body size to successfully hunt seals, scavenging the carcasses from other bears' kills is an important source of nutrition. Subadults may also be forced to accept a half-eaten carcass if they kill a seal but cannot defend it from larger polar bears. After feeding, polar bears wash themselves with water or snow.<sup id="cite_ref-behavior_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-behavior-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although polar bears are extraordinarily powerful, its primary prey species, the ringed seal, is much smaller than itself, and many of the seals hunted are pups rather than adults. Ringed seals are born weighing 5.4&nbsp;kg (12&nbsp;lb) and grown to an estimated average weight of only 60&nbsp;kg (130&nbsp;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup> They also in places prey heavily upon the <a href="/wiki/Harp_seal" title="Harp seal">harp seal</a> (<i>Pagophilus groenlandicus</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Harbor_seal" title="Harbor seal">harbour seal</a> (<i>Phoca vitulina</i>), or the <a href="/wiki/Hooded_seal" title="Hooded seal">hooded seal</a> (<i>Cystophora cristata</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-animaldiversity.org_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-animaldiversity.org-98">[98]</a></sup> The bearded seal, on the other hand, can be nearly the same size as the bear itself, averaging 270&nbsp;kg (600&nbsp;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup> Adult male bearded seals, at 350 to 500&nbsp;kg (770 to 1,100&nbsp;lb) are too large for a female bear to overtake, and so are potential prey only for mature male bears.<sup id="cite_ref-overtake_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-overtake-99">[99]</a></sup> Large males also occasionally attempt to hunt and kill even larger prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> It can kill an adult <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a> (<i>Odobenus rosmarus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OverwinteringWalruses_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OverwinteringWalruses-101">[101]</a></sup> although this is rarely attempted. At up to 2,000&nbsp;kg (4,400&nbsp;lb) and a typical adult mass range of 600 to 1,500&nbsp;kg (1,300 to 3,300&nbsp;lb), a walrus can be more than twice the bear's weight,<sup id="cite_ref-Clarkson1994_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson1994-102">[102]</a></sup> has extremely thick skin and has up to 1-metre (3&nbsp;ft)-long ivory tusks that can be used as formidable weapons. A polar bear may charge a group of walruses, with the goal of separating a young, infirm, or injured walrus from the pod. They will even attack adult walruses when their diving holes have frozen over or intercept them before they can get back to the diving hole in the ice. Yet, polar bears will very seldom attack full-grown adult walruses, with the largest male walrus probably invulnerable unless otherwise injured or incapacitated. Since an attack on a walrus tends to be an extremely protracted and exhausting venture, bears have been known to back down from the attack after making the initial injury to the walrus.<sup id="cite_ref-OverwinteringWalruses_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OverwinteringWalruses-101">[101]</a></sup> Polar bears have also been seen to prey on <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">beluga whales</a> (<i>Delphinapterus leucas</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Narwhal" title="Narwhal">narwhals</a> (<i>Monodon monoceros</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup> by swiping at them at breathing holes. The whales are of similar size to the walrus and nearly as difficult for the bear to subdue.<sup id="cite_ref-Macdonald_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macdonald-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Most terrestrial animals in the Arctic can outrun the polar bear on land as polar bears overheat quickly, and most marine animals the bear encounters can outswim it. In some areas, the polar bear's diet is supplemented by walrus calves and by the carcasses of dead adult walruses or whales, whose blubber is readily devoured even when rotten.<sup id="cite_ref-bruemmer2533_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruemmer2533-80">[80]</a></sup> Polar bears sometimes swim underwater to catch fish like the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_charr" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic charr">Arctic charr</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Fourhorn_sculpin" title="Fourhorn sculpin">fourhorn sculpin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg/220px-Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg/330px-Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg/440px-Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="524" data-file-height="673"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_posture.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some characteristic postures: <div><ol><li>at rest;</li><li>assessing a situation;</li><li>when feeding</li></ol></div></div></div></div>
<p>With the exception of pregnant females, polar bears are active year-round, although they have a vestigial <a href="/wiki/Hibernation_induction_trigger" class="mw-redirect" title="Hibernation induction trigger">hibernation induction trigger</a> in their blood. Unlike brown and black bears, polar bears are capable of <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> for up to several months during late summer and early fall, when they cannot hunt for seals because the sea is unfrozen.<sup id="cite_ref-tick_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tick-105">[105]</a></sup> When sea ice is unavailable during summer and early autumn, some populations live off fat reserves for months at a time,<sup id="cite_ref-stirling1988_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1988-106">[106]</a></sup> as polar bears do not 'hibernate' any time of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Being both curious animals and scavengers,<sup id="cite_ref-Clarkson1994_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson1994-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manning_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manning-108">[108]</a></sup> polar bears investigate and consume <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">garbage</a> where they come into contact with humans.<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clarkson1994_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson1994-102">[102]</a></sup> Polar bears may attempt to consume almost anything they can find, including hazardous substances such as <a href="/wiki/Expanded_polystyrene" class="mw-redirect" title="Expanded polystyrene">styrofoam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Car_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Car battery">car batteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fluid" title="Hydraulic fluid">hydraulic fluid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Motor_oil" title="Motor oil">motor oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarkson1994_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson1994-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Manning_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manning-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">dump</a> in <a href="/wiki/Churchill,_Manitoba" title="Churchill, Manitoba">Churchill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> was closed in 2006 to protect bears, and waste is now recycled or transported to <a href="/wiki/Thompson,_Manitoba" title="Thompson, Manitoba">Thompson, Manitoba</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eliasson_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eliasson-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_flexibility">Dietary flexibility</span></h4>
<p>Although seal predation is the primary and an indispensable way of life for most polar bears, when alternatives are present they are quite flexible. Polar bears consume a wide variety of other wild foods, including <a href="/wiki/Muskox" title="Muskox">muskox</a> (<i>Ovibos moschatus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> (<i>Rangifer tarandus</i>), birds, eggs, <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a><sup id="cite_ref-animaldiversity.org_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-animaldiversity.org-98">[98]</a></sup> and other polar bears. They may also eat plants, including <a href="/wiki/Berry" title="Berry">berries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-111">[111]</a></sup> however, none of these have been a significant part of their diet,<sup id="cite_ref-Clarkson1994_102-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson1994-102">[102]</a></sup> except for beachcast <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammal</a> carcasses. Given the change in climate, with ice breaking up in areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> earlier than it used to, polar bears are exploiting food resources such as <a href="/wiki/Snow_goose" title="Snow goose">snow geese</a> and eggs, and plants such as <a href="/wiki/Lyme_grass" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyme grass">lyme grass</a> in increased quantities.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>When stalking land animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Muskox" title="Muskox">muskox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-111">[111]</a></sup> and even <a href="/wiki/Willow_ptarmigan" title="Willow ptarmigan">willow ptarmigan</a> (<i>Lagopus lagopus</i>), polar bears appear to make use of vegetative cover and wind direction to bring them as close to their prey as possible before attacking. Polar bears have been observed to hunt the small <a href="/wiki/Svalbard_reindeer" title="Svalbard reindeer">Svalbard reindeer</a> (<i>R. t. platyrhynchus</i>), which weigh only 40 to 60&nbsp;kg (90 to 130&nbsp;lb) as adults, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Barren-ground_caribou" title="Barren-ground caribou">barren-ground caribou</a> (<i>R. t. groenlandicus</i>), which is about twice as heavy as the former.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brook_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brook-114">[114]</a></sup> Adult muskox, which can weigh 450&nbsp;kg (1,000&nbsp;lb) or more, are a more formidable quarry.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a> are not typical prey, the killing of one during the summer months can greatly increase the odds of survival during that lean period. Like the brown bear, most ungulate prey of polar bears is likely to be young, sickly or injured specimens rather than healthy adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Brook_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brook-114">[114]</a></sup> The polar bear's <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> is specialized to require large amounts of fat from marine mammals, and it cannot derive sufficient caloric intake from terrestrial food.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In their southern range, especially near <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Bay" title="James Bay">James Bay</a>, Canadian polar bears endure all summer without sea ice to hunt from.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-111">[111]</a></sup> Here, their food ecology shows their dietary flexibility. They still manage to consume some seals, but they are food-deprived in summer as only marine mammal carcasses are an important alternative without sea ice, especially carcasses of the <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">beluga whale</a>. These alternatives may reduce the rate of weight loss of bears when on land.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> One scientist found that 71% of the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> bears had fed on seaweed (<a href="/wiki/Marine_algae" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine algae">marine algae</a>) and that about half were feeding on birds<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_97-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-97">[97]</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Dovekie" class="mw-redirect" title="Dovekie">dovekie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_ducks" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea ducks">sea ducks</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_duck" title="Long-tailed duck">long-tailed duck</a> (53%) and <a href="/wiki/Common_eider" title="Common eider">common eider</a>, by swimming underwater to catch them. They were also diving to feed on <a href="/wiki/Blue_mussel" title="Blue mussel">blue mussels</a> and other underwater food sources like the <a href="/wiki/Strongylocentrotus_droebachiensis" title="Strongylocentrotus droebachiensis">green sea urchin</a>. 24% had eaten <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">moss</a> recently, 19% had consumed grass, 34% had eaten <a href="/wiki/Black_crowberry" class="mw-redirect" title="Black crowberry">black crowberry</a> and about half had consumed <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-111">[111]</a></sup> This study illustrates the polar bear's dietary flexibility but it does not represent its life history elsewhere. Most polar bears elsewhere will never have access to these alternatives, except for the marine mammal carcasses that are important wherever they occur.
</p><p>In Svalbard, polar bears were observed to kill <a href="/wiki/White-beaked_dolphin" title="White-beaked dolphin">white-beaked dolphins</a> during spring, when the dolphins were trapped in the sea ice. The bears then proceeded to <a href="/wiki/Hoarding_(animal_behavior)" title="Hoarding (animal behavior)">cache</a> the carcasses, which remained and were eaten during the ice-free summer and autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_lifecycle">Reproduction and lifecycle</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg/220px-Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg/330px-Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg/440px-Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="643"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_us_fish.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cubs are born helpless and typically nurse for two and a half years</div></div></div>
<p>Courtship and mating take place on the sea ice in April and May, when polar bears congregate in the best seal hunting areas.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup> A male may follow the tracks of a breeding female for 100&nbsp;km (60&nbsp;mi) or more, and after finding her engage in intense fighting with other males over mating rights, fights that often result in scars and broken teeth.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup> Polar bears have a generally <a href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_animals" title="Polygyny in animals">polygynous</a> mating system; recent genetic testing of mothers and cubs, however, has uncovered cases of litters in which cubs have different fathers.<sup id="cite_ref-carpenter_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carpenter-121">[121]</a></sup> Partners stay together and mate repeatedly for an entire week; the mating ritual <a href="/wiki/Induced_ovulation_(animals)" title="Induced ovulation (animals)">induces ovulation</a> in the female.<sup id="cite_ref-rosing42-48_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosing42-48-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>After mating, the fertilized egg remains in a suspended state until August or September. During these four months, the pregnant female eats prodigious amounts of food, gaining at least 200&nbsp;kg (440&nbsp;lb) and often more than doubling her body weight.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternity_denning_and_early_life">Maternity denning and early life</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Female_polar_bear_(Ursus_maritimus)_with_cub,_Svalbard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Female_polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_cub%2C_Svalbard.jpg/220px-Female_polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_cub%2C_Svalbard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Female_polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_cub%2C_Svalbard.jpg/330px-Female_polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_cub%2C_Svalbard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Female_polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_cub%2C_Svalbard.jpg/440px-Female_polar_bear_%28Ursus_maritimus%29_with_cub%2C_Svalbard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4041" data-file-height="2273"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Female_polar_bear_(Ursus_maritimus)_with_cub,_Svalbard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother and cub on <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, Norway</div></div></div>
<p>When the ice floes are at their minimum in the fall, ending the possibility of hunting, each pregnant female digs a <i>maternity den</i> consisting of a narrow entrance tunnel leading to one to three chambers. Most maternity dens are in snowdrifts, but may also be made underground in <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> if it is not sufficiently cold yet for snow.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup> In most subpopulations, maternity dens are situated on land a few kilometres from the coast, and the individuals in a subpopulation tend to reuse the same denning areas each year.<sup id="cite_ref-derocher2004_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derocher2004-37">[37]</a></sup> The polar bears that do not den on land make their dens on the sea ice. In the den, she enters a dormant state similar to <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernation</a>. This hibernation-like state does not consist of continuous sleeping; however, the bear's heart rate slows from 46 to 27 beats per minute.<sup id="cite_ref-lockwood17-21_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockwood17-21-123">[123]</a></sup> Her body temperature does not decrease during this period as it would for a typical mammal in hibernation.<sup id="cite_ref-stirling1988_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1988-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between November and February, cubs are born blind, covered with a light down fur, and weighing less than 0.9&nbsp;kg (2.0&nbsp;lb),<sup id="cite_ref-rosing42-48_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosing42-48-122">[122]</a></sup> but in captivity they might be delivered in the earlier months. The earliest recorded birth of polar bears in captivity was on 11 October 2011 in the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Zoo" title="Toronto Zoo">Toronto Zoo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> On average, each litter has two cubs. The family remains in the den until mid-February to mid-April, with the mother maintaining her fast while nursing her cubs on a fat-rich milk. By the time the mother breaks open the entrance to the den, her cubs weigh about 10 to 15 kilograms (22 to 33&nbsp;lb). For about 12 to 15 days, the family spends time outside the den while remaining in its vicinity, the mother grazing on vegetation while the cubs become used to walking and playing. Then they begin the long walk from the denning area to the sea ice, where the mother can once again catch seals. Depending on the timing of ice-floe breakup in the fall, she may have fasted for up to eight months.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup> During this time, cubs playfully imitate the mother's hunting methods in preparation for later life.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><span class="mw-tmh-player video"><video id="mwe_player_2_placeholder" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cub_polar_bear_is_nursing_2.ogv/220px--Cub_polar_bear_is_nursing_2.ogv.jpg" preload="none" class="thumbimage" width="220" height="167" data-durationhint="38" data-mwtitle="Cub_polar_bear_is_nursing_2.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" disabled="disabled" tabindex="-1" style="aspect-ratio: 220 / 167;"></video><a class="mw-tmh-play" href="/wiki/File:Cub_polar_bear_is_nursing_2.ogv" title="Play media" role="button"><span class="mw-tmh-play-icon"></span></a><span class="mw-tmh-duration mw-tmh-label" aria-label="38 seconds">0:38</span></span>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cub_polar_bear_is_nursing_2.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cub nursing</div></div></div><p>Female polar bears have been known to adopt other cubs. Multiple cases of adoption of wild cubs have been confirmed by genetic testing.<sup id="cite_ref-malen2015_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malen2015-127">[127]</a></sup> Adult bears of either sex occasionally kill and eat polar bear cubs.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> As of 2006, in Alaska, 42% of cubs were reaching 12 months of age, down from 65% in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-regehr2006_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-regehr2006-130">[130]</a></sup> In most areas, cubs are <a href="/wiki/Weaning" title="Weaning">weaned</a> at two and a half years of age, when the mother chases them away or abandons them. The Western Hudson Bay subpopulation is unusual in that its female polar bears sometimes wean their cubs at only one and a half years.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup> This was the case for 40% of cubs there in the early 1980s; however by the 1990s, fewer than 20% of cubs were weaned this young.<sup id="cite_ref-stirling1999_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1999-131">[131]</a></sup> After the mother leaves, sibling cubs sometimes travel and share food together for weeks or months.<sup id="cite_ref-bruemmer2533_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruemmer2533-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h4>
<p>Females begin to breed at the age of four years in most areas, and five years in the area of the Beaufort Sea. Males usually reach sexual maturity at six years; however, as competition for females is fierce, many do not breed until the age of eight or ten.<sup id="cite_ref-reproduction_120-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reproduction-120">[120]</a></sup> A study in Hudson Bay indicated that both the reproductive success and the maternal weight of females peaked in their mid-teens. Maternal success appeared to decline after this point, possibly because of an age-related impairment in the ability to store the fat necessary to rear cubs.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polar bears appear to be less affected by infectious diseases and parasites than most terrestrial mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-pbi_survival_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbi_survival-133">[133]</a></sup> Polar bears are especially susceptible to <i><a href="/wiki/Trichinella" title="Trichinella">Trichinella</a></i>, a parasitic roundworm they contract through <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">cannibalism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> although infections are usually not fatal. Only one case of a polar bear with <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> has been documented, even though polar bears frequently interact with <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic foxes</a>, which often carry rabies.<sup id="cite_ref-pbi_survival_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbi_survival-133">[133]</a></sup> Bacterial <a href="/wiki/Leptospirosis" title="Leptospirosis">leptospirosis</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Morbillivirus" title="Morbillivirus">Morbillivirus</a></i> have been recorded. Polar bears sometimes have problems with various <a href="/wiki/Skin_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Skin disease">skin diseases</a> that may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a> or other parasites.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_expectancy">Life expectancy</span></h4>
<p>Polar bears rarely live beyond 25 years.<sup id="cite_ref-hemstock29-35_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hemstock29-35-135">[135]</a></sup> The oldest wild bears on record died at age 32, whereas the oldest captive was a female who died in 1991, age 43.<sup id="cite_ref-wrigley_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wrigley-136">[136]</a></sup> The causes of death in wild adult polar bears are poorly understood, as carcasses are rarely found in the species's frigid habitat. In the wild, old polar bears eventually become too weak to catch food, and gradually starve to death. Polar bears injured in fights or accidents may either die from their injuries, or become unable to hunt effectively, leading to starvation.<sup id="cite_ref-pbi_survival_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbi_survival-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_role">Ecological role</span></h3>
<p>The polar bear is the <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a> within its range, and is a <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a> for the Arctic.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Several animal species, particularly Arctic foxes (<i>Vulpes lagopus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Glaucous_gull" title="Glaucous gull">glaucous gulls</a> (<i>Larus hyperboreus</i>), routinely scavenge polar bear kills.<sup id="cite_ref-behavior_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-behavior-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relationship between ringed seals and polar bears is so close that the abundance of ringed seals in some areas appears to regulate the density of polar bears, while polar bear predation in turn regulates density and reproductive success of ringed seals.<sup id="cite_ref-amstrup2007_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amstrup2007-92">[92]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">evolutionary pressure</a> of polar bear predation on seals probably accounts for some significant differences between Arctic and Antarctic seals. Compared to the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctic</a>, where there is no major surface predator, Arctic seals use more breathing holes per individual, appear more restless when hauled out on the ice, and rarely defecate on the ice. The baby fur of most Arctic seal species is white, presumably to provide camouflage from predators, whereas Antarctic seals all have dark fur at birth.<sup id="cite_ref-behavior_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-behavior-75">[75]</a></sup>
