</p><p>Brown bears tend to dominate polar bears in disputes over carcasses,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> and dead polar bear cubs have been found in brown bear dens.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">Wolves</a> are rarely encountered by polar bears, though there are two records of <a href="/wiki/Arctic_wolf" title="Arctic wolf">Arctic wolf</a> (<i>Canis lupus arctos</i>) packs killing polar bear cubs.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Adult polar bears are occasionally vulnerable to predation by <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a> (<i>Orcinus orca</i>) while swimming, but they are rarely reported as taken and bears are likely to avoid entering the water if possible if they detect an orca pod in the area. The melting sea ice in the Arctic may be causing an increase of orcas in the Arctic sea, which may increase the risk of predation on polar bears but also may benefit the bears by providing more whale carcasses that they can scavenge.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The remains of polar bears have been found in the stomachs of large <a href="/wiki/Greenland_shark" title="Greenland shark">Greenland sharks</a> (<i>Somniosus microcephalus</i>), although it certainly cannot be ruled out that the bears were merely scavenged by this slow-moving, unusual shark.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> A rather unlikely killer of a grown polar bear has reportedly included a <a href="/wiki/Wolverine" title="Wolverine">wolverine</a> (<i>Gulo gulo</i>), anecdotally reported to have suffocated a bear in a zoo with a bite to the throat during a conflict. This report may well be dubious, however.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Polar bears are sometimes the host of arctic mites such as <i><a href="/wiki/Alaskozetes_antarcticus" title="Alaskozetes antarcticus">Alaskozetes antarcticus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-behavior_75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-behavior-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-distance_swimming_and_diving">Long-distance swimming and diving</span></h3>
<p>Researchers tracked 52 sows in the southern Beaufort Sea off Alaska with GPS system collars; no boars were involved in the study due to males' necks being too thick for the GPS-equipped collars. Fifty long-distance swims were recorded; the longest at 354 kilometres (220&nbsp;mi), with an average of 155 kilometres (96&nbsp;mi). The length of these swims ranged from most of a day to ten days. Ten of the sows had a cub swim with them and after a year, six cubs survived. The study did not determine if the others lost their cubs before, during, or some time after their long swims. Researchers do not know whether or not this is a new behaviour; before polar ice shrinkage, they opined that there was probably neither the need nor opportunity to swim such long distances.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>The polar bear may swim underwater for up to three minutes to approach seals on shore or on ice floes.<sup id="cite_ref-Stirling_&amp;_van_Meurs_2015_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stirling_&amp;_van_Meurs_2015-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting">Hunting</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_people">Indigenous people</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg/220px-Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg/330px-Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg/440px-Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2701" data-file-height="1758"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenland-polarbear-skin_hg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skins of hunted bears</div></div></div>
<p>Polar bears have long provided important raw materials for Arctic peoples, including the Inuit, <a href="/wiki/Yupik_peoples" title="Yupik peoples">Yupik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_people" title="Chukchi people">Chukchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nenets_people" title="Nenets people">Nenets</a>, Russian <a href="/wiki/Pomors" title="Pomors">Pomors</a> and others. Hunters commonly used teams of dogs to distract the bear, allowing the hunter to spear the bear or shoot it with arrows at closer range.<sup id="cite_ref-original_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-original-149">[149]</a></sup> Almost all parts of captured animals had a use.<sup id="cite_ref-lw6-9_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw6-9-150">[150]</a></sup> The fur was used in particular to make trousers and, by the Nenets, to make galoshes-like outer footwear called <span title="uncoded-language romanization"><i lang="mis-Latn">tobok</i></span>; the <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a> is edible, despite some risk of <a href="/wiki/Trichinosis" title="Trichinosis">trichinosis</a>; the fat was used in food and as a fuel for lighting homes, alongside seal and whale blubber; <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">sinews</a> were used as thread for sewing clothes; the <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Myocardium" class="mw-redirect" title="Myocardium">heart</a> were dried and powdered for medicinal purposes; the large canine teeth were highly valued as <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">talismans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup> Only the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> was not used, as its high concentration of <a href="/wiki/Retinol" title="Retinol">vitamin A</a> is poisonous. As a carnivore, which feeds largely upon <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>-eating carnivores, the polar bear ingests large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Retinol" title="Retinol">vitamin A</a> that is stored in their <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">livers</a>. The resulting high concentrations cause <a href="/wiki/Hypervitaminosis_A" title="Hypervitaminosis A">hypervitaminosis A</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Hunters make sure to either toss the liver into the sea or bury it in order to spare their dogs from potential poisoning.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup> Traditional subsistence hunting was on a small enough scale to not significantly affect polar bear populations, mostly because of the sparseness of the human population in polar bear habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-lw31-36_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw31-36-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_commercial_harvest">History of commercial harvest</span></h3>
<p>In Russia, polar bear furs were already being commercially traded in the 14th century, though it was of low value compared to Arctic fox or even reindeer fur.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup> The growth of the human population in the Eurasian Arctic in the 16th and 17th century, together with the advent of firearms and increasing trade, dramatically increased the harvest of polar bears.<sup id="cite_ref-stirling1988_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1988-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nwt_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwt-154">[154]</a></sup> However, since polar bear fur has always played a marginal commercial role, data on the historical harvest is fragmentary. It is known, for example, that already in the winter of 1784/1785 Russian Pomors on <a href="/wiki/Spitsbergen" title="Spitsbergen">Spitsbergen</a> harvested 150 polar bears in <a href="/wiki/Magdalenefjorden" title="Magdalenefjorden">Magdalenefjorden</a>. In the early 20th century, Norwegian hunters were harvesting 300 bears per year at the same location. Estimates of total historical harvest suggest that from the beginning of the 18th century, roughly 400 to 500 animals were being harvested annually in northern Eurasia, reaching a peak of 1,300 to 1,500 animals in the early 20th century, and falling off as the numbers began dwindling.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first half of the 20th century, mechanized and overpoweringly efficient methods of hunting and trapping came into use in North America as well. Polar bears were chased from <a href="/wiki/Snowmobile" title="Snowmobile">snowmobiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>, and airplanes, the latter practice described in a 1965 <i>New York Times</i> editorial as being "about as sporting as machine gunning a cow."<sup id="cite_ref-bruemmer93-111_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruemmer93-111-155">[155]</a></sup> Norwegians used "self-killing guns", comprising a loaded rifle in a baited box that was placed at the level of a bear's head, and which fired when the string attached to the bait was pulled.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The numbers taken grew rapidly in the 1960s, peaking around 1968 with a global total of 1,250 bears that year.<sup id="cite_ref-PBSG2_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSG2-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_regulations">Contemporary regulations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg/220px-Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg/330px-Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg/440px-Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2570" data-file-height="1712"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar-Bear-Warning-Longyearbyen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Road sign warning about the presence of bears. The Norwegian text translates into "Applies to all of Svalbard".</div></div></div>
<p>Concerns over the future survival of the species led to the development of national regulations on polar bear hunting, beginning in the mid-1950s. The Soviet Union banned all hunting in 1956. Canada began imposing hunting quotas in 1968. Norway passed a series of increasingly strict regulations from 1965 to 1973, and has completely banned hunting since then. The United States began regulating hunting in 1971 and adopted the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Mammal_Protection_Act_of_1972" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972">Marine Mammal Protection Act</a> in 1972. In 1973, the <a href="/wiki/International_Agreement_on_the_Conservation_of_Polar_Bears" class="mw-redirect" title="International Agreement on the Conservation of Polar Bears">International Agreement on the Conservation of Polar Bears</a> was signed by all five nations whose territory is inhabited by polar bears: Canada, Denmark, Norway, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and the United States. Member countries agreed to place restrictions on recreational and commercial hunting, ban hunting from aircraft and icebreakers, and conduct further research.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The treaty allows hunting "by local people using traditional methods". Norway is the only country of the five in which all harvest of polar bears is banned. The agreement was a rare case of international cooperation during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Biologist <a href="/wiki/Ian_Stirling_(biologist)" title="Ian Stirling (biologist)">Ian Stirling</a> commented, "For many years, the conservation of polar bears was the only subject in the entire Arctic that nations from both sides of the Iron Curtain could agree upon sufficiently to sign an agreement. Such was the intensity of human fascination with this magnificent predator, the only marine bear."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>Agreements have been made between countries to co-manage their shared polar bear subpopulations. After several years of negotiations, Russia and the United States signed an agreement in October 2000 to jointly set quotas for indigenous subsistence hunting in Alaska and <a href="/wiki/Chukotka_Autonomous_Okrug" title="Chukotka Autonomous Okrug">Chukotka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> The treaty was ratified in October 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> In September 2015, the polar bear range states agreed upon a "circumpolar action plan" describing their conservation strategy for polar bears.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The species is listed in Appendix II of the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_International_Trade_in_Endangered_Species" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species">Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species</a> (CITES) meaning international trade, including in parts or derivatives, is controlled by the CITES system of permits and certificates.<sup id="cite_ref-CITES_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CITES-3">[3]</a></sup> The United States government has proposed that polar bears be transferred to Appendix I of <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a> which would ban all commercial international trade in polar bear parts.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The decision to leave the species listed under Appendix II was endorsed by the IUCN and <a href="/wiki/TRAFFIC" class="mw-redirect" title="TRAFFIC">TRAFFIC</a>, who determined that such an uplisting was unlikely to confer a conservation benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dogsledquebec.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dogsledquebec.jpg/220px-Dogsledquebec.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dogsledquebec.jpg/330px-Dogsledquebec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dogsledquebec.jpg/440px-Dogsledquebec.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dogsledquebec.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dogsled" class="mw-redirect" title="Dogsled">Dogsleds</a> are used for recreational hunting of polar bears in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a></div></div></div>
<p>Polar bears were designated "Not at Risk" in April 1986 and uplisted to "Special Concern" in April 1991. This status was re-evaluated and confirmed in April 1999, November 2002, and April 2008. Polar bears continue to be listed as a species of special concern in Canada because of their sensitivity to overharvest and because of an expected range contraction caused by loss of Arctic sea ice.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than 600 bears are killed per year by humans across Canada,<sup id="cite_ref-pbsg2017_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbsg2017-48">[48]</a></sup> a rate calculated by scientists to be unsustainable for some areas, notably <a href="/wiki/Baffin_Bay" title="Baffin Bay">Baffin Bay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PBI_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBI-38">[38]</a></sup> Canada has allowed sport hunters accompanied by local guides and dog-sled teams since 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> but the practice was not common until the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-wenzel2005_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wenzel2005-167">[167]</a></sup> The guiding of sport hunters provides meaningful employment and an important source of income for northern communities in which economic opportunities are few.<sup id="cite_ref-campbell_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-46">[46]</a></sup> Sport hunting can bring CDN$20,000 to $35,000 per bear into northern communities, which until recently has been mostly from American hunters.<sup id="cite_ref-nunavut_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nunavut-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The territory of <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a> accounts for the location 80% of annual kills in Canada. In 2005, the government of Nunavut increased the quota from 400 to 518 bears,<sup id="cite_ref-nunavut_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nunavut-168">[168]</a></sup> despite protests from the IUCN Polar Bear Specialist Group.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> In two areas where harvest levels have been increased based on increased sightings, science-based studies have indicated declining populations, and a third area is considered data-deficient.<sup id="cite_ref-sd2007_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd2007-170">[170]</a></sup> While most of that quota is hunted by the indigenous Inuit, a growing share is sold to recreational hunters. (0.8% in the 1970s, 7.1% in the 1980s, and 14.6% in the 1990s)<sup id="cite_ref-wenzel2005_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wenzel2005-167">[167]</a></sup> Nunavut polar bear biologist, <a href="/wiki/Mitchell_Taylor" title="Mitchell Taylor">Mitchell Taylor</a>, who was formerly responsible for polar bear conservation in the territory, has insisted that bear numbers are being sustained under current hunting limits.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-171">[171]</a></sup> In 2010, the 2005 increase was partially reversed. Government of Nunavut officials announced that the polar bear quota for the Baffin Bay region would be gradually reduced from 105 per year to 65 by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-nunatsiaq_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nunatsiaq-172">[172]</a></sup> The Government of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a> maintain their own quota of 72 to 103 bears within the <a href="/wiki/Inuvialuit" title="Inuvialuit">Inuvialuit</a> communities of which some are set aside for sports hunters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Environment_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Environment Canada">Environment Canada</a> also banned the export from Canada of fur, claws, skulls and other products from polar bears harvested in Baffin Bay as of 1 January 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-nunatsiaq_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nunatsiaq-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the way polar bear hunting quotas are managed in Canada, attempts to discourage sport hunting would actually increase the number of bears killed in the short term. Canada allocates a certain number of permits each year to sport and subsistence hunting, and those that are not used for sport hunting are re-allocated to indigenous subsistence hunting. Whereas northern communities kill all the polar bears they are permitted to take each year, only half of sport hunters with permits actually manage to kill a polar bear. If a sport hunter does not kill a polar bear before his or her permit expires, the permit cannot be transferred to another hunter.<sup id="cite_ref-campbell_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2011, <a href="/wiki/Environment_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Environment Canada">Environment Canada</a> published a national polar bear conservation strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland">Greenland</span></h4>
<p>In Greenland, hunting restrictions were first introduced in 1994 and expanded by executive order in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-PBSG14_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSG14-42">[42]</a></sup> Until 2005 Greenland placed no limit on hunting by indigenous people. However, in 2006 it imposed a limit of 150, while also allowed recreational hunting for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Other provisions included year-round protection of cubs and mothers, restrictions on weapons used and various administrative requirements to catalogue kills.<sup id="cite_ref-PBSG14_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSG14-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span></h4>
<p>Polar bears were hunted heavily in Svalbard, Norway throughout the 19th century and to as recently as 1973, when the conservation treaty was signed. 900 bears a year were harvested in the 1920s and after World War II, there were as many as 400–500 harvested annually. Some regulations of hunting did exist. In 1927, poisoning was outlawed while in 1939, certain denning sights were declared off limits. The killing of females and cubs was made illegal in 1965. Killing of polar bears decreased somewhat 25–30 years before the treaty. Despite this, the polar bear population continued to decline and by 1973, only around 1000 bears were left in Svalbard. Only with the passage of the treaty did they begin to recover.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h4>
<p>The Soviet Union banned the harvest of polar bears in 1956; however, <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a> continued, and is estimated to pose a serious threat to the polar bear population.<sup id="cite_ref-PBSG14_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSG14-42">[42]</a></sup> In recent years, polar bears have approached coastal villages in Chukotka more frequently due to the shrinking of the sea ice, endangering humans and raising concerns that illegal hunting would become even more prevalent. In 2007, the Russian government made subsistence hunting legal for indigenous Chukotkan peoples only, a move supported by Russia's most prominent bear researchers and the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a> as a means to curb poaching.<sup id="cite_ref-myers_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-myers-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polar bears are currently listed as "Rare", of "Uncertain Status", or "Rehabilitated and rehabilitating" in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Data_Book_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Data Book of Russia">Red Data Book of Russia</a>, depending on population.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Natural_Resources_and_Environment_(Russia)" title="Ministry of Natural Resources and Environment (Russia)">Ministry of Natural Resources and Environment</a> published a strategy for polar bear conservation in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Marine_Mammal_Protection_Act_of_1972" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972">Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972</a> afforded polar bears some protection in the United States. It banned hunting (except by indigenous subsistence hunters), banned importing of polar bear parts (except polar bear pelts taken legally in Canada), and banned the harassment of polar bears. On 15 May 2008, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">United States Department of the Interior</a> listed the polar bear as a threatened species under the <a href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Endangered Species Act">Endangered Species Act</a>, citing the melting of Arctic sea ice as the primary threat to the polar bear.<sup id="cite_ref-ecos.fws.gov_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecos.fws.gov-179">[179]</a></sup> It banned all importing of polar bear trophies. Importing products made from polar bears had been prohibited from 1972 to 1994 under the Marine Mammal Protection Act, and restricted between 1994 and 2008. Under those restrictions, permits from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fish_and_Wildlife_Service" title="United States Fish and Wildlife Service">United States Fish and Wildlife Service</a> were required to import sport-hunted polar bear trophies taken in hunting expeditions in Canada. The permit process required that the bear be taken from an area with quotas based on sound management principles.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Since 1994, hundreds of sport-hunted polar bear trophies have been imported into the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> In 2015, the U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service published a draft conservation management plan for polar bears to improve their status under the Endangered Species Act and the Marine Mammal Protection Act.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg/220px-Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg/330px-Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg/440px-Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5152" data-file-height="3864"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_bear_in_Denver_Zoo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Polar bear in Denver Zoo.</div></div></div>
<h2><span id="Conservation_status.2C_threats.2C_and_controversies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_status,_threats,_and_controversies">Conservation status, threats, and controversies</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear_Habitat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Polar_Bear_Habitat.png/330px-Polar_Bear_Habitat.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Polar_Bear_Habitat.png/495px-Polar_Bear_Habitat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Polar_Bear_Habitat.png/660px-Polar_Bear_Habitat.png 2x" data-file-width="981" data-file-height="879"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear_Habitat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Geological Survey">U.S. Geological Survey</a> shows projected changes in polar bear habitat from 2001 to 2010 and 2041 to 2050. Red areas indicate loss of optimal polar bear habitat; blue areas indicate gain.</div></div></div>
<p>Polar bear population sizes and trends are difficult to estimate accurately because they occupy remote home ranges and exist at low population densities. Polar bear fieldwork can also be hazardous to researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> As of 2015, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) reports that the global population of polar bears is 22,000 to 31,000, and the current population trend is unknown. Nevertheless, polar bears are listed as "Vulnerable" under criterion A3c, which indicates an expected population decrease of ≥30% over the next three generations (~34.5 years) due to "decline in area of occupancy, extent of occurrence and/or quality of habitat". Risks to the polar bear include <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Toxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic">toxic</a> contaminants, conflicts with shipping, oil and gas exploration and development, and human-bear interactions including harvesting and possible stresses from recreational polar-bear watching.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>, the polar bear is important as an <a href="/wiki/Indicator_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Indicator species">indicator</a> of Arctic <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_health" title="Ecosystem health">ecosystem health</a>. Polar bears are studied to gain understanding of what is happening throughout the Arctic, because at-risk polar bears are often a sign of something wrong with the Arctic <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">Climate change in the Arctic</a></div>
<p>The key danger for polar bears posed by the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change" title="Effects of climate change">effects of climate change</a> is malnutrition or starvation due to <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat loss</a>. Polar bears hunt seals from a platform of sea ice. Rising temperatures cause the sea ice to melt earlier in the year, driving the bears to shore before they have built sufficient fat reserves to survive the period of scarce food in the late summer and early fall.<sup id="cite_ref-stirling1999_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1999-131">[131]</a></sup> Reduction in sea-ice cover also forces bears to swim longer distances, which further depletes their energy stores and occasionally leads to <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drowning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-monnett2006_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monnett2006-185">[185]</a></sup> Thinner sea ice tends to deform more easily, which appears to make it more difficult for polar bears to access seals.<sup id="cite_ref-amstrup2007_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amstrup2007-92">[92]</a></sup> Insufficient nourishment leads to lower reproductive rates in adult females and lower survival rates in cubs and juvenile bears, in addition to poorer body condition in bears of all ages.<sup id="cite_ref-derocher2004_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derocher2004-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Climate_Impact_Assessment" title="Arctic Climate Impact Assessment">Arctic Climate Impact Assessment</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> and many leading polar bear biologists have expressed grave concerns about the impact of climate change, with some predicting extinction by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg/220px-Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg/330px-Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg/440px-Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2954" data-file-height="1980"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ursus_maritimus_Polar_bear_with_cub_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mothers and cubs have high nutritional requirements, which are not met if the seal-hunting season is too short</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to creating nutritional stress, a warming climate is expected to affect various other aspects of polar bear life: changes in sea ice affect the ability of pregnant females to build suitable maternity dens.<sup id="cite_ref-Schliebe_2006_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schliebe_2006-31">[31]</a></sup> As the distance increases between the pack ice and the coast, females must swim longer distances to reach favoured denning areas on land. Thawing of permafrost would affect the bears who traditionally den underground, and warm winters could result in den roofs collapsing or having reduced insulative value. For the polar bears that currently den on multi-year ice, increased ice mobility may result in longer distances for mothers and young cubs to walk when they return to seal-hunting areas in the spring.<sup id="cite_ref-derocher2004_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derocher2004-37">[37]</a></sup> Disease-causing <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasites</a> would flourish more readily in a warmer climate.<sup id="cite_ref-amstrup2007_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amstrup2007-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Problematic interactions between polar bears and humans, such as foraging by bears in garbage dumps, have historically been more prevalent in years when ice-floe breakup occurred early and local polar bears were relatively thin. Increased human-bear interactions, including fatal attacks on humans, are likely to increase as the sea ice shrinks and hungry bears try to find food on land.<sup id="cite_ref-stirling2006_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling2006-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg/220px-Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg/330px-Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg/440px-Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3009" data-file-height="1693"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Endangered_arctic_-_starving_polar_bear.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starving bear near Svalbard</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change" title="Effects of climate change">effects of climate change</a> are most profound in the southern part of the polar bear's range, and this is indeed where significant degradation of local populations has been observed.<sup id="cite_ref-acia_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acia-188">[188]</a></sup> The Western Hudson Bay subpopulation, in a southern part of the range, also happens to be one of the best-studied polar bear subpopulations. This subpopulation feeds heavily on ringed seals in late spring, when newly weaned and easily hunted seal pups are abundant. The late spring hunting season ends for polar bears when the ice begins to melt and break up, and they fast or eat little during the summer until the sea freezes again.<sup id="cite_ref-sd2007_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd2007-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to warming air temperatures, ice-floe breakup in western Hudson Bay is currently occurring three weeks earlier than it did 30 years ago, reducing the duration of the polar bear feeding season. The body condition of polar bears has declined during this period; the average weight of lone (and likely pregnant) female polar bears was approximately 290&nbsp;kg (640&nbsp;lb) in 1980 and 230&nbsp;kg (510&nbsp;lb) in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-sd2007_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd2007-170">[170]</a></sup> Between 1987 and 2004, the Western Hudson Bay population declined by 22%,<sup id="cite_ref-regehr2007_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-regehr2007-189">[189]</a></sup> although the population was listed as "stable" as of 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-pbsg2017_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbsg2017-48">[48]</a></sup> As the climate change melts sea ice, the U.S. Geological Survey projects that two-thirds of polar bears will disappear by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-WWF_2016_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WWF_2016-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Alaska, the effects of <a href="/wiki/Arctic_shrinkage" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic shrinkage">sea ice shrinkage</a> have contributed to higher mortality rates in polar bear cubs, and have led to changes in the denning locations of pregnant females.<sup id="cite_ref-regehr2006_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-regehr2006-130">[130]</a></sup> The proportion of maternity dens on sea ice has changed from 62% between the years 1985 through 1994, to 37% over the years 1998 through 2004. Thus, now the Alaskan population more resembles the world population in that it is more likely to den on land.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> In recent years, polar bears in the Arctic have undertaken longer than usual swims to find prey, possibly resulting in four recorded drownings in the unusually large ice pack regression of 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-monnett2006_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monnett2006-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>A new development is that polar bears have begun ranging to new territory. While not unheard of but still uncommon, polar bears have been sighted increasingly in larger numbers ashore, staying on the mainland for longer periods of time during the summer months, particularly in North Canada, traveling farther inland.<sup id="cite_ref-harvey_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvey-192">[192]</a></sup> This may cause an increased reliance on terrestrial diets, such as goose eggs, waterfowl and caribou,<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> as well as increased human–bear conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-harvey_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvey-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span></h3>
<p>Polar bears <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">accumulate</a> high levels of <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyl</a> (PCBs) and chlorinated <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a>. Due to their position at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_pyramid" title="Ecological pyramid">ecological pyramid</a>, with a diet heavy in <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a> in which halocarbons concentrate, their bodies are among the most contaminated of Arctic mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-ec_bulletin_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec_bulletin-194">[194]</a></sup> Halocarbons (also known as organohalogens) are known to be toxic to other animals, because they mimic <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormone</a> chemistry, and <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">biomarkers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_G" title="Immunoglobulin G">immunoglobulin G</a> and retinol suggest similar effects on polar bears. PCBs have received the most study, and they have been associated with birth defects and immune system deficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many chemicals, such as PCBs and <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>, have been internationally banned due to the recognition of their harm on the environment. Their concentrations in polar bear tissues continued to rise for decades after being banned, as these chemicals spread through the food chain. Since then, the trend seems to have abated, with tissue concentrations of PCBs declining between studies performed from 1989 to 1993 and studies performed from 1996 to 2002. During the same time periods, DDT was found to be notably lower in the Western Hudson Bay population only.<sup id="cite_ref-Verreault_2005_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verreault_2005-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_and_gas_development">Oil and gas development</span></h3>
<p>Oil and gas development in polar bear habitat can affect the bears in a variety of ways. An <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spill</a> in the Arctic would most likely concentrate in the areas where polar bears and their prey are also concentrated, such as sea ice leads.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup> Because polar bears rely partly on their fur for insulation and soiling of the fur by oil reduces its insulative value, oil spills put bears at risk of dying from <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a>. Polar bears exposed to oil spill conditions have been observed to lick the oil from their fur, leading to fatal kidney failure.<sup id="cite_ref-tick_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tick-105">[105]</a></sup> Maternity dens, used by pregnant females and by females with infants, can also be disturbed by nearby oil exploration and development. Disturbance of these sensitive sites may trigger the mother to abandon her den prematurely, or abandon her litter altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions">Predictions</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Steven_Amstrup" title="Steven Amstrup">Steven Amstrup</a> and other <a href="/wiki/U.S._Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Geological Survey">U.S. Geological Survey</a> scientists have predicted two-thirds of the world's polar bears may disappear by 2050, based on moderate projections for the shrinking of summer sea ice caused by climate change,<sup id="cite_ref-amstrup2007_92-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amstrup2007-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WWF_2016_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WWF_2016-190">[190]</a></sup> though the validity of this study has been debated.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Amstrup_et_al._2009._198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amstrup_et_al._2009.-198">[198]</a></sup> The bears could disappear from Europe, Asia, and Alaska, and be depleted from the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Arctic_Archipelago" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Arctic Archipelago">Canadian Arctic Archipelago</a> and areas off the northern Greenland coast. By 2080, they could disappear from Greenland entirely and from the northern Canadian coast, leaving only dwindling numbers in the interior Arctic Archipelago.<sup id="cite_ref-amstrup2007_92-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amstrup2007-92">[92]</a></sup> However, in the short term, some polar bear populations in historically colder regions of the Arctic may temporarily benefit from a milder climate, as multiyear ice that is too thick for seals to create breathing holes is replaced by thinner annual ice.<sup id="cite_ref-Amstrup_2011_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amstrup_2011-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polar bears diverged from brown bears 400,000–600,000 years ago and have survived past periods of climate fluctuation. It has been claimed that polar bears will be able to adapt to terrestrial food sources as the sea ice they use to hunt seals disappears.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> However, most polar bear biologists think that polar bears will be unable to completely offset the loss of calorie-rich seal blubber with terrestrial foods, and that they will be outcompeted by brown bears in this terrestrial niche, ultimately leading to a population decline.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_over_species_protection">Controversy over species protection</span></h3>

.mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290"></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2019</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Warnings about the future of the polar bear are often contrasted with the fact that worldwide population estimates have increased over the past 50 years and are relatively stable today.<sup id="cite_ref-usfwsa_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usfwsa-202">[202]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WWF_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WWF-203">[203]</a></sup> Some estimates of the global population are around 5,000 to 10,000 in the early 1970s;<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-204">[204]</a></sup> other estimates were 20,000 to 40,000 during the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-distribution_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distribution-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stirling1988_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stirling1988-106">[106]</a></sup> Current estimates put the global population at between 20,000 and 25,000<sup id="cite_ref-PBSG14_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSG14-42">[42]</a></sup> or 22,000 and 31,000.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_status_19_November_2021_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn_status_19_November_2021-2">[2]</a></sup> Despite the encouraging rebound of some populations, there is little evidence to suggest polar bears are thriving overall.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are several reasons for the apparent discordance between past and projected population trends: estimates from the 1950s and 1960s were based on stories from explorers and hunters rather than on scientific surveys.<sup id="cite_ref-increasing_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-increasing-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Second, controls of harvesting were introduced that allowed this previously overhunted species to recover. Third, the recent effects of climate change have affected sea ice abundance in different areas to varying degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-increasing_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-increasing-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p>Debate over the listing of the polar bear under endangered species legislation has put conservation groups and Canada's <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> at opposing positions;<sup id="cite_ref-campbell_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-46">[46]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a> government and many northern residents have condemned the U.S. initiative to list the polar bear under the Endangered Species Act.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Many Inuit believe the polar bear population is increasing, and restrictions on commercial sport-hunting are likely to lead to a loss of income to their communities.<sup id="cite_ref-campbell_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrf_wenzel_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrf_wenzel-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg/220px-PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="74" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg/330px-PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg/440px-PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2046" data-file-height="686"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PolarBearWalrusTuskCarving.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving, made by <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_people" title="Chukchi people">Chukchi</a> carvers in the 1940s on a <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a> tusk, depicts polar bears hunting walrus</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_folklore">Indigenous folklore</span></h3>
<p>For the indigenous peoples of the Arctic, polar bears have long played an important cultural and material role.<sup id="cite_ref-lw6-9_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw6-9-150">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup> Polar bear remains have been found at hunting sites dating to 2,500 to 3,000 years ago<sup id="cite_ref-lw31-36_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lw31-36-153">[153]</a></sup> and 1,500-year-old <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave paintings</a> of polar bears have been found in the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Peninsula" title="Chukchi Peninsula">Chukchi Peninsula</a>. Indeed, it has been suggested that Arctic peoples' skills in seal hunting and <a href="/wiki/Igloo" title="Igloo">igloo</a> construction has been in part acquired from the polar bears themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Natives" title="Alaska Natives">Alaska Natives</a> have many <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folk tales</a> featuring the bears including legends in which bears are humans when inside their own houses and put on bear hides when going outside, and stories of how the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> that is said to resemble a great bear surrounded by dogs came into being. These legends reveal a deep respect for the polar bear, which is portrayed as both spiritually powerful and closely akin to humans. The human-like posture of bears when standing and sitting, and the resemblance of a skinned bear carcass to the human body, have probably contributed to the belief that the spirits of humans and bears were interchangeable.<sup id="cite_ref-original_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-original-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_people" title="Chukchi people">Chukchi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Yupik" title="Siberian Yupik">Yupik</a> of eastern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, there was <a href="/wiki/Shamanism_among_Eskimo_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamanism among Eskimo peoples">a longstanding shamanistic ritual</a> of "thanksgiving" to the hunted polar bear. After killing the animal, its head and skin were removed and cleaned and brought into the home, and a feast was held in the hunting camp in its honor. To appease the spirit of the bear, traditional song and drum music was played, and the skull was ceremonially fed and offered a pipe.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Only once the spirit was appeased was the skull be separated from the skin, taken beyond the bounds of the homestead, and placed in the ground, facing north.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Nenet" class="mw-redirect" title="Nenet">Nenets</a> of north-central Siberia placed particular value on the talismanic power of the prominent <a href="/wiki/Canine_teeth" class="mw-redirect" title="Canine teeth">canine teeth</a>. These were traded in the villages of the lower <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khatanga_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Khatanga River">Khatanga</a> rivers to the forest-dwelling peoples further south, who would sew them into their hats as protection against <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bears</a>. It was believed that the "little nephew" (the brown bear) would not dare to attack a man wearing the tooth of its powerful "big uncle", the polar bear. The skulls of killed polar bears were buried at sacred sites, and altars, called <i>sedyangi</i>, were constructed out of the skulls. Several such sites have been preserved on the <a href="/wiki/Yamal_Peninsula" title="Yamal Peninsula">Yamal Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Uspensky_151-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uspensky-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols_and_mascots">Symbols and mascots</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_(1911).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_%281911%29.jpg/220px-GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_%281911%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_%281911%29.jpg/330px-GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_%281911%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_%281911%29.jpg/440px-GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_%281911%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4453" data-file-height="2988"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GRE-10-Greenland-5_Kroner_(1911).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenland's 1911 five kroner note depicting a polar bear</div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg/200px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="252" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg/300px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg/400px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Chukotka.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="823" data-file-height="1039"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Coat of arms of the <a href="/wiki/Chukotka_Autonomous_Okrug" title="Chukotka Autonomous Okrug">Chukotka Autonomous Okrug</a> in the Russian Federation</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg/200px-Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="287" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg/300px-Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg/400px-Coat_of_arms_of_Greenland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="574"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Coat of arms of the Greenlandic Self-Rule government (<a href="/wiki/Kalaallit_Nunaat" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalaallit Nunaat">Kalaallit Nunaat</a>)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Their distinctive appearance and their association with the Arctic have made polar bears popular icons, especially in those areas where they are native. The Canadian <a href="/wiki/Toonie" title="Toonie">two-dollar coin</a> carries an image of a lone polar bear on its reverse side, while a special millennium edition featured three.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Vehicle licence plates in the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_the_Northwest_Territories" title="Vehicle registration plates of the Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a> in Canada are in the shape of a polar bear, as was the case in <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_Nunavut" title="Vehicle registration plates of Nunavut">Nunavut</a> until 2012; these now display polar bear artwork instead.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> The polar bear is the mascot of <a href="/wiki/Bowdoin_College" title="Bowdoin College">Bowdoin College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>; the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alaska_Fairbanks" title="University of Alaska Fairbanks">University of Alaska Fairbanks</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/1988_Winter_Olympics" title="1988 Winter Olympics">1988 Winter Olympics</a> held in <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eisb%C3%A4ren_Berlin" title="Eisbären Berlin">Eisbären Berlin</a> hockey team uses a roaring polar bear as their logo, and the <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a> hockey team the <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Checkers" title="Charlotte Checkers">Charlotte Checkers</a> uses a polar bear named Chubby Checker as their mascot.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> has used images of the polar bear in its advertising,<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Polar_Beverages" title="Polar Beverages">Polar Beverages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nelvana" title="Nelvana">Nelvana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bundaberg_Rum" title="Bundaberg Rum">Bundaberg Rum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Klondike_bar" title="Klondike bar">Klondike bars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fox%27s_Glacier_Mints" title="Fox's Glacier Mints">Fox's Glacier Mints</a> all feature polar bears in their logos.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiction">Fiction</span></h3>
<p>Polar bears are popular in fiction, particularly in books for children or teenagers. 
</p>
<ul><li><i>The Polar Bear Son</i> is adapted from a <a href="/wiki/Inuit_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Inuit mythology">traditional Inuit tale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup></li>
<li>The animated television series <i><a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Island" title="Noah's Island">Noah's Island</a></i> features a polar bear named Noah as the protagonist.</li>
<li>Polar bears feature prominently in <i><a href="/wiki/East_(novel)" title="East (novel)">East</a></i> (<i>North Child</i> in the UK) by <a href="/wiki/Edith_Pattou" title="Edith Pattou">Edith Pattou</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>The Bear</i> by <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Briggs" title="Raymond Briggs">Raymond Briggs</a> (adapted into <a href="/wiki/The_Bear_(1998_film)" title="The Bear (1998 film)">an animated short in 1998</a>) is a story of a girl's encounter with a polar bear.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Panserbjorne" class="mw-redirect" title="Panserbjorne">panserbjørne</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Philip_Pullman" title="Philip Pullman">Philip Pullman</a>'s fantasy trilogy <i><a href="/wiki/His_Dark_Materials" title="His Dark Materials">His Dark Materials</a></i> are sapient, dignified, <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a> polar bears. They feature prominently in the 2007 <a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Compass_(film)" title="The Golden Compass (film)">film adaptation of <i>The Golden Compass</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></li>
<li>The television series <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_(TV_series)" title="Lost (TV series)">Lost</a></i> features polar bears living on the tropical island setting.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></li>
<li>The manga and anime series <i><a href="/wiki/Shirokuma_Cafe" title="Shirokuma Cafe">Shirokuma Cafe</a></i> centers on a polar bear who runs a cafe in Japan.</li></ul>

(1998). "Is polar bear hair fiber optic?". <i>Applied Optics</i>. <b>37</b> (15): 3198–3200. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1998ApOpt..37.3198K">1998ApOpt..37.3198K</a>. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1364%2FAO.37.003198">10.1364/AO.37.003198</a>. <a href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a>&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18273269">18273269</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Applied+Optics&amp;rft.atitle=Is+polar+bear+hair+fiber+optic%3F&amp;rft.volume=37&amp;rft.issue=15&amp;rft.pages=3198-3200&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F18273269&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1364%2FAO.37.003198&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1998ApOpt..37.3198K&amp;rft.aulast=Koon&amp;rft.aufirst=Daniel+W.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APolar+bear" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In unusually warm conditions, the hollow tubes provide an excellent home for algae. While the algae is harmless to the bears, it is often a worry to the zoos housing them, and affected animals are sometimes washed in a salt solution, or mild <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">peroxide bleach</a> to make the fur white again.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Bruemmer">Bruemmer</a>, p. 101. In a meeting of the five circumpolar nations on 6 September 1965, estimates of the worldwide population ranged from 5,000 to 19,000. "The truth was, no one knew... Scientific research had been sketchy and knowledge of the polar bear was based largely on stories brought back by explorers and hunters."</span>
